The Maxim: One Person Cannot Act as Agent for Both Sides
Legal maxim prohibiting dual capacity in agency for marriage contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule prohibiting dual capacity in contracts (agent for both parties, or agent for one and principal for the other) and apply it to marriage agency scenarios.

Core Idea
A single person cannot legally represent both parties in a contract, nor act as agent for one and principal for the other.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim of Hanafi contract law, specifically in agency (wakalah) for marriage. The rule prevents conflicts of interest and ensures each party's interests are independently represented. Understanding this maxim is essential for analyzing the validity of agency structures in Islamic transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited capacities
Read the maxim: a single person cannot be an agent (fuduli) on both sides, nor an agent on one side and a principal (asil) on the other.
Fuduli means an agent acting without prior authorization, but here it applies to any agency capacity.
2
Understand the legal reasoning
The prohibition is due to inherent conflict of interest: one person cannot serve two masters with opposing interests.
In marriage, the groom and bride have different interests; one representative cannot fairly serve both.
3
Memorize the maxim
Commit the Arabic text to memory: إِنَّ الْوَاحِدَ لَا يَصْلُحُ فُضُولِيًّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ...
This is a key maxim in the chapter of Wakalah bi al-Nikah.
4
Apply the maxim
Given a scenario, determine if the person's capacity violates the maxim. If yes, the contract is invalid.
The ruling is according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two shaykhs).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْوَاحِدَ لَا يَصْلُحُ فُضُولِيًّا مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ أَوْ فُضُولِيًّا مِنْ جَانِبِ وَأَصِيلًا مِنْ جَانِبِ عِندَ الطَّرَفَيْنِ
Indeed, a single person is not suitable as an agent (fuduli) from both sides, nor as an agent from one side and a principal from the other side, according to both [Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf? Muhammad?].
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage states a legal maxim (qawida) concerning agency in the chapter on Wakalah bi al-Nikah (agency in marriage). It establishes that one individual cannot serve as an agent for both parties in a contract (e.g., representing both the groom and the bride) nor can he be an agent for one side and a principal on the other. This would create a conflict of interest and is invalid. The maxim applies according to the two shaykhs (the two main students of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), though there may be differences in other chapters.

Key Terms
فُضُولِي (fuduli): An intermediary or agent who acts on behalf of another, often used in the context of contracts without prior authorization.
أَصِيل (asil): The principal or the party on whose behalf the agent acts; here meaning acting directly as a party to the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, can one person act as agent for both the groom and the bride in a marriage contract?
A) Yes, if both parties agree
B) No, never
C) Yes, but only in non-marriage contracts
D) It depends on the school

Key Takeaway
A single person cannot legally represent both parties in a contract, nor act as agent for one and principal for the other; doing so renders the contract invalid.

Mahr Fixed After Contract: Abu Yusuf's View
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahr specified post-contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Yusuf's position on mahr specified after the marriage contract and differentiate it from the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Core Idea
When mahr is fixed after nikah, Abu Yusuf treats it as if fixed within; the Sahibayn do not.

This lesson presents a concise intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of specifying mahr after the marriage contract. The maxim attributed to Imam Abu Yusuf asserts that such a post-contract specification carries the same legal force as if it had been stipulated within the contract itself. This position contrasts with that of the other two major Hanafi authorities, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, who hold that the default mahr al-mithl applies in the absence of specification during the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputing parties
Note that the maxim is explicitly attributed to Abu Yusuf. The opposing position is held by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn).
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf occurs when the three main imams differ.
2
Understand the maxim's content
The maxim states that a mahr fixed after the contract (al-mafrud ba'd al-'aqd) is legally equivalent to one fixed within the contract (al-mafrud fi al-'aqd). This means the wife is entitled to the specified amount.
3
Contrast with the opposing view
The Sahibayn do not give effect to a post-contract specification; instead, the wife receives mahr al-mithl (the standard dowry suitable for her status).
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference is the amount the wife receives: under Abu Yusuf's view, the specified amount; under the Sahibayn, mahr al-mithl (which may be different).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْمَفْرُوضَ بَعْدَ الْعَقْدِ كَالْمَفْرُوضِ فِى الْعَقْدِ عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ
Indeed, that which is fixed (as mahr) after the contract is like that which is fixed in the contract, according to Abu Yusuf.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This maxim from the chapter on Mahr presents the position of Imam Abu Yusuf that if the mahr amount is determined after the marriage contract has been concluded, it is legally equivalent to having been stipulated within the contract itself. This implies that the wife is entitled to that specified mahr, not the mahr al-mithl (standard dowry). The other authorities (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) likely hold a different view, considering the mahr al-mithl as the default if not specified initially.

Key Terms
مَفْرُوض (mafrud): The mahr (dower) that is specifically stipulated or agreed upon.
مَهْر الْمِثْل (mahr al-mithl): The standard dowry suitable for a woman of similar status, used when no specific mahr is mentioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In this maxim, whose view is that a mahr fixed after the contract is like one fixed within it?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) All three

Key Takeaway
Abu Yusuf considers post-contract mahr specification valid; the Sahibayn do not, defaulting to mahr al-mithl.

Marriage Not Invalidated by Corrupt Conditions
Effect of invalid conditions on marriage vs. sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a corrupt condition in marriage does not invalidate the contract, and distinguish this from the case of sale.

Core Idea
Marriage stands despite a corrupt condition; sale is invalidated.

This passage presents a key maxim in Hanafi fiqh: the leniency of marriage compared to the strictness of sale. In marriage, a condition that contradicts the nature of the contract (shart fasid) is void, but the marriage itself remains valid. This is contrary to sale, where such a condition would render the sale void. Understanding this principle helps in distinguishing between contracts that are intended for permanence (marriage) versus those that are transactional (sale).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contracts involved
Recognize that the maxim contrasts marriage (nikah) with sale (bay').
2
State the ruling for each contract
For marriage: the contract remains valid and the corrupt condition is void. For sale: the entire contract becomes void.
This is the key difference.
3
Understand the underlying reason
Marriage is founded on leniency (samaha) and permanence, so extraneous conditions do not undermine its core validity. Sale is based on strictness and precision, so any contradictory condition vitiates the contract.
This reason helps in applying the maxim to new cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ النِّكَاحَ لَا يَبْطُلُ بِالشُّرُوْطِ الْفَاسِدَةِ بِخِلَافِ الْبَيْعِ
Indeed, marriage is not invalidated by corrupt conditions, unlike sale.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This maxim builds on the previous one. Because marriage is founded on leniency, the inclusion of a corrupt condition (shart fasid) does not nullify the marriage contract; the condition is void but the marriage stands. In contrast, sale is strict, so a corrupt condition invalidates the sale contract. For example, a condition that the husband must not take a second wife is void, but the marriage remains valid.

Key Terms
شَرْط فَاسِد (shart fasid): A condition that is invalid or corrupt from a legal perspective, e.g., one that contradicts the nature of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to a marriage contract if it includes a corrupt condition?
A) It becomes invalid
B) The condition is void but marriage stands
C) The marriage becomes suspended
D) The husband must pay extra mahr

Key Takeaway
Marriage is not invalidated by corrupt conditions; such conditions are void but the contract remains valid.

Nikah Cannot Be Annulled After Completion
Intra-Hanafi ruling on annulment after completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a completed marriage contract cannot be annulled (faskh) and explain why faskh is distinct from talaq.

Core Idea
A completed marriage contract cannot be annulled; only talaq dissolves it.

The passage states a fundamental Hanafi principle: once a valid marriage contract is fully concluded with offer, acceptance, and conditions, it cannot be annulled (faskh) by either party or a judge. This distinguishes marriage from other contracts. The only ways to end the marriage are through talaq (divorce), khul' (divorce by redemption), or judicial decree on specific grounds like defects. This lesson examines the implications of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and ruling
The subject is the marriage contract (nikah). The ruling: it does not accept faskh (annulment) after completion (tamam al-aqd).
Faskh here means voiding the contract ab initio.
2
Distinguish faskh from talaq
Faskh annuls the contract from the beginning (as if never existed). Talaq dissolves it from the moment of pronouncement forward. The Hanafi rule forbids faskh but allows talaq.
3
Understand the condition 'after completion'
The ruling applies after all prerequisites (offer, acceptance, witnesses) are met. Before completion (e.g., during the option of puberty for minors), faskh may be possible.
Hanafis allow faskh in cases like defect or apostasy via judicial decree, but these are exceptions.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a husband says 'I annul this marriage' without using talaq, the marriage remains intact. He must use a clear talaq formula or initiate khul'.
Intent does not change the ruling; the proper legal mechanism is required.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ النِّكَاحَ لَا يَقْبَلُ الْفَسْخَ عِنْدَنَا بَعْدَ تَمَامِ الْعَقْدِ
Indeed, marriage does not accept annulment (faskh) according to us after the completion of the contract.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
Once a valid marriage contract is fully concluded (with offer and acceptance and conditions), it cannot be annulled (faskh) by either party or by a judge. The only way to end the marriage is through divorce (talaq) or through khul', or by certain grounds like defects. This is the Hanafi position (عندنا). Some other schools allow annulment in certain cases.

Key Terms
الفسخ (al-faskh): Annulment; dissolving a contract ab initio, as if it never existed.
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage contract.
تمام العقد (tamam al-aqd): Completion of the contract; after all conditions are met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, after a marriage contract is completed, can it be annulled (faskh)?
A) Yes, at any time.
B) No, only talaq is possible.
C) Yes, but only with mutual consent.
D) Only by a judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a fully concluded marriage contract cannot be annulled; it must be dissolved through talaq or equivalent means.

Marriage Contract as Legal Substitute for Intercourse
A foundational legal principle (qa'ida) in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that a valid marriage contract stands in the place of actual intercourse in certain legal contexts and identify two applications of this principle.

Core Idea
A valid marriage contract alone can trigger rulings normally caused by intercourse—the contract suffices where consummation is presumed.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain legal effects are typically contingent upon actual intercourse. However, the law recognizes that in some contexts, the marriage contract itself—even without consummation—is sufficient to produce those same effects. This lesson examines the principle that nikah (marriage) is considered equivalent to wati (intercourse) 'in the place where intercourse is conceptualized'—that is, where the law presumes consummation. Understanding this principle is key to rules on iddah, lineage, and mahram relationships.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Arabic phrase
Commit the exact Arabic to memory: إِنَّ النِّكَاحَ يُقَامُ مَقَامَ الْوَطْيِ فِي مَوْضِعِ التَّصَوُّرِ
This is a foundational qa'ida in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Understand the lexical meaning
'Yuqam maqam' means 'stands in place'; 'mawdi' al-tasawwar' means 'context where intercourse is conceptually assumed or presumed.'
The principle applies only where the law presumes potential intercourse, not universally.
3
Identify the legal effect
The principle means that a valid marriage contract triggers rulings that normally require actual intercourse, such as iddah after death or divorce, and establishment of mahram relationships.
The contract substitutes for physical consummation in these specific contexts.
4
Recall two applications
1) Iddah: a woman must observe iddah after husband's death or divorce even if not consummated. 2) Prohibition due to relationship: marriage creates mahramiyya as if consummation occurred.
These are classic examples from Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ النِّكَاحَ يُقَامُ مَقَامَ الْوَطْيِ فِي مَوْضِعِ التَّصَوُّرِ
Indeed, marriage is set in place of intercourse in the place of conceptualization (where intercourse is assumed).
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
In certain legal contexts where intercourse is considered possible or presumed, the marriage contract itself suffices to bring about rulings similar to actual intercourse. For example, if a man marries a woman but does not consummate, still some iddah rulings apply as if consummation occurred, like the necessity of iddah even without physical union in some cases? Actually, this principle applies to situations like when a man marries a woman who was previously married to his son? Complex. The key is that in certain rulings (like establishing lineage or iddah), the contract of marriage can be treated as equivalent to actual intercourse for legal purposes.

Key Terms
الوطي (al-wati): Sexual intercourse.
موضع التصور (mawdi' al-tasawwar): Place where intercourse is conceptually possible or presumed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle 'al-nikah yuqam maqam al-wati fi mawdi' al-tasawwar', which of the following is correct?
A) Marriage contract has no effect without intercourse.
B) Marriage contract can substitute for intercourse in all matters.
C) Marriage contract can substitute for intercourse in contexts where consummation is presumed.
D) Only actual intercourse triggers iddah.

Key Takeaway
The marriage contract itself, because it legally presumes potential consummation, suffices to trigger certain rulings such as iddah, even without actual intercourse.

Does Consummation Transfer to a Subsequent Marriage?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on transfer of consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of the Shaykhayn and Imam Muhammad on whether consummation in a first marriage counts as consummation in a second marriage, and explain the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) of this intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Consummation in a prior marriage transfers to a subsequent marriage according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

This short passage from the Usool al-Hidayah presents a concise intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of consummation across marriages. The core question: if a woman consummates a marriage, then divorces and remarries, does the prior consummation carry legal weight in the new marriage? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (al-Shaykhayn) affirm that it does, while Imam Muhammad dissents. This seemingly narrow difference has significant implications for rulings on mahr, iddah, and family relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
The passage addresses whether consummation (dukhul) in a first marriage can be legally considered as consummation in a subsequent marriage for the same woman.
This is a hukm wad'i (declaratory ruling) about legal attribution.
2
State the Position of the Shaykhayn
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that dukhul in the first marriage counts as dukhul in the second marriage.
This is the majority view within the Hanafi school.
3
State the Opposing Position of Imam Muhammad
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that consummation does not transfer; each marriage is independent.
His view is based on the principle that legal effects are tied to the specific contract.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
The dispute leads to concrete differences. For example, if a woman divorces from her second husband without consummation, according to Shaykhayn she is entitled to full mahr and must observe iddah as if consummation occurred. According to Muhammad, she receives half mahr and no iddah.
This affects iddah, mahr, and prohibitions like marriage to the woman's mother.
5
Assess Authority and Tarjih
The passage notes that this is a dispute within Zahir al-Riwayah. Later Hanafi works often adopt the Shaykhayn's view, but tarjih is not explicitly given here.
Students should be aware that in practice, the view of Shaykhayn is often relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الدُّخُولَ فِي النِّكَاحِ الْأَوَّلِ يَكُونُ دُخُولًا فِي النِّكَاحِ التَّانِي عِنْدَ الشَّيْخَيْنِ خِلَافًا لِمُحَمَّدٍ
Indeed, entry (consummation) in the first marriage is considered entry in the second marriage according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), contrary to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This usool addresses a scenario where a woman marries a man, consummates, then divorces, and later marries another man. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the prior consummation in the first marriage is treated as if consummation occurred in the second marriage for certain rulings, like establishing iddah or mahr. Imam Muhammad disagrees, holding that consummation in the first marriage does not transfer to the second.

Key Terms
الدخول (al-dukhul): Consummation of marriage; sexual intercourse.
الشيخين (al-Shaykhayn): The two masters: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Sometimes includes Muhammad? Here it's Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf only.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who holds that consummation in the first marriage counts as consummation in the second marriage?
A) Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
B) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
C) All three imams
D) Only Imam Muhammad

Key Takeaway
The Shaykhayn view that consummation transfers across marriages is the relied-upon opinion in many Hanafi texts, but the dispute remains within the school.

The Right of Custody Derives from the Mother's Side
This passage states the source of custody rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that the right of custody (hadana) is derived from the mother's side and explain the priority order among maternal relatives.

Core Idea
Custody begins with the mother—her right is derived from her natural nurturing role, not from the father.

This passage establishes a foundational principle of Hanafi family law: the right to raise and care for a child (hadana) originates from the mother and her maternal line. It is not initially a paternal right. Understanding this principle is crucial for resolving custody disputes in fiqh. Let us unpack its meaning and application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term: وِلَايَة حِصَانَة الْوَلَد
This term refers to the authority of custody. Note that it is a right (wilaya) to care for and raise the child.
The term appears in the first part of the Arabic passage.
2
Understand the verb 'tustafad' (تُسْتَفَادُ)
It means 'is derived' or 'is obtained'. The right is not automatically assumed; it is derived from a specific source: the mothers' side.
The passive form emphasizes that the source is the mothers.
3
Recall the Hanafi priority order for custody
The mother is first, then her mother (maternal grandmother), then maternal aunts, etc. The father does not have primary custody initially.
This principle is based on the mother's natural nurturing role and is supported by hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ وِلَايَةَ حِصَانَةِ الْوَلَدِ تُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الْأُمَّهَاتِ
Indeed, the authority of custody of the child is derived from the side of the mothers.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This usool states the Hanafi position that the right to custody (hadana/wilayat al-hadana) of a child primarily belongs to the mother and her female relatives. It is not derived from the father's side initially. The mother has the first right, then her mother, then sisters, etc. This is based on the mother's natural nurturing role.

Key Terms
وِلَايَة حِصَانَة الْوَلَد (wilayat hisanat al-walad): The legal authority to raise and care for a child (custody).
تستفاد من قبل الأمهات (tustafad min qibal al-ummahat): Derived from the mothers' side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi usool in this lesson, from where is the right of custody derived?
A) From the father's side.
B) From the mothers' side.
C) From both parents equally.
D) From the closest relative by blood regardless of gender.

Key Takeaway
The right of custody (hadana) in Hanafi fiqh is derived from the mother's side, making her and her female maternal relatives the primary custodians.

The Source of Custody Authority: Mothers
Legal maxim on custody of child

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim regarding the source of custody authority and identify the mother as the primary custodian for young children.

Core Idea
Custody authority (hadanah) derives from mothers, not fathers — a foundational Hanafi family law maxim.

This lesson introduces a foundational maxim in Hanafi family law: the authority of child custody (hadanah) is obtained from the mother's side. While both parents have roles, the mother's right to physical custody takes precedence for young children. This maxim underlies many detailed rulings in the chapter on child custody.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim
Locate the Arabic phrase that states the legal principle: 'إِنَّ وِلَايَةَ حِصَانَةِ الْوَلَدِ تُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الْأُمَّهَاتِ'.
This is a clear statement of the rule.
2
Understand the terms
Recognize that 'wilayat hadanah' means the legal authority to care for and raise the child, and this authority is attributed to the mothers.
Hadith and Qur'an support the mother's primary role.
3
Apply the principle
Use the maxim to determine that in custody disputes, the mother is entitled to custody as long as she meets the conditions of eligibility (e.g., sanity, trustworthiness).
This is the starting point for further rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حضانة الولد ومن أحق به
(١١٤) إِنَّ وِلَايَةَ حِصَانَةِ الْوَلَدِ تُسْتَفَادُ مِنْ قِبَلِ الْأُمَّهَاتِ
Chapter of Custody of the Child and Who is More Entitled to Him (114) Indeed, the authority of custody of the child is obtained from the side of the mothers.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This is a ruling statement establishing a fundamental principle in Hanafi family law: the right of custody (hadanah) primarily derives from the mother. The word 'wilayah' here refers to the legal authority to care for and raise the child. This maxim is the basis for the rule that the mother is the primary custodian for young children, as long as she meets the conditions of eligibility. The chapter heading indicates the topic: who is most entitled to custody.

Key Terms
ولاية حضانة (wilāyat ḥaḍānah): The legal authority and responsibility for the custody and upbringing of a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this maxim, from whom is the authority of custody of a child obtained?
A) The father
B) The mother
C) The grandfather
D) The state

Key Takeaway
The authority of child custody (hadanah) is obtained from the mother's side, making her the primary custodian in Hanafi fiqh.

6 min
One Person as Agent for Both Parties in Marriage and Sale
Explanation of a Hanafi legal maxim on agency in marriage vs. sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why one agent can represent both parties in a marriage contract but not in a sale contract according to Hanafi fiqh, and state the underlying rationale based on the attachment of rights.

Core Idea
In sale, rights attach to the agent, creating a contradiction; in marriage, they do not, so one agent for both sides is valid.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between marriage and sale regarding agency from both sides. This passage from Al-Hidayah presents the principle and its rationale: because in sale the agent becomes personally liable for the rights and obligations, having the same person represent both buyer and seller creates a legal impossibility. In marriage, the rights such as dower are the direct responsibility of the parties, not the agent, so one person can validly represent both the groom and the bride. We will explore this ruling, its proof, and its application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon: agency in marriage from both sides (i.e., one person acting as agent for both the groom and the bride). The ruling is that it is valid (sahih), unlike in sale.
The Arabic term used is ' يَتَوَلَّى طَرَفَي النِّكَاحِ' – one person oversees both sides of marriage.
2
Understand the proof (dalil) through the underlying rationale
The author provides a rational proof: in sale, all rights (ḥuqūq) revert to the agent, making him both demander and demanded. This impossibility (bāṭil) invalidates dual agency in sale. In marriage, rights such as dower do not attach to the agent, so no contradiction arises.
The 'illah (effective cause) is the attachment of rights to the agent.
3
Apply the principle to a concrete example
Consider the case where a man says to a woman 'zawwijnī' (marry me). She acts as principal from her side and agent from his side, thus representing both parties. The marriage is valid because the principle applies.
This example is directly from the Hidayah passage.
4
Compare with the opposing view (if any)
The passage notes that other schools may differ. The Hanafi position is based on the nature of rights, while other schools might have different criteria for agency validity.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.

Book of Marriage (1) Indeed one person can oversee both sides of marriage unlike sale. Translation: One person can become the overseer of both sides of marriage unlike sale. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that one person can become an agent from both sides in marriage, i.e., from the man and the woman, unlike sale where one person cannot become an agent from both sides, i.e., from the seller and the buyer. Because in sale all rights revert to the agent, e.g., handling the sale, receiving payment, etc., are the agent's responsibility. If one person becomes agent from both seller and buyer, then one person would become the demander and the demanded, and one person being both demander and demanded is void. Therefore, agency from both sides in sale is void. In other words, the agent in sale is both the one who makes ownership and the one who becomes owner. But in marriage it is not so, because in marriage rights do not revert to the agent; e.g., paying the dower is not the agent's responsibility; rather, the woman will demand the dower from her husband herself. Hence, if one person becomes agent from both sides in marriage, the impossibility of one person being both demander and demanded does not arise. Therefore, agency from both sides in marriage is valid. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to explain that if a man says to a woman 'zawwijnī' (marry me), and then the woman performs the marriage of herself to him, the marriage is contracted. Because in this case the man made the woman his agent in marriage, i.e., by saying 'zawwijnī' he asked her to act as principal from her side and agent from his side and marry him to herself. Thus, this is agency in marriage from both sides, and agency from both sides in marriage is valid, so the marriage is contracted. Al-Hidayah: Book of Marriage, vol. 2 p. 325, Rahmani print. And the difference between marriage and sale is that in sale the rights attach to the agent, and the agent in marriage is not...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim from the Hanafi school: one person can serve as agent for both parties in a marriage contract but not in a sale contract. The text opens by stating the principle in Arabic, then provides a detailed Urdu commentary explaining the rationale. The key distinction lies in the nature of rights: in a sale, the agent becomes responsible for executing the contract and handling payments, so having the same person represent both seller and buyer would create a contradiction where the agent is simultaneously demanding and being demanded. In marriage, however, the rights and obligations (like dower) do not fall on the agent but directly on the parties themselves; the agent merely facilitates the offer and acceptance. Therefore, one person can validly represent both the man and the woman. The author of Hidayah illustrates this with an example: if a man tells a woman 'zawwijnī' (marry me), she can marry him to herself because she acts as principal from her own side and agent from his side. The passage concludes with a direct quote from Al-Hidayah that reiterates the difference: in sale, rights relate to the agent; in marriage, they do not. This commentary guards against a common misunderstanding that agency rules are uniform across contracts, highlighting instead that the structure of rights determines validity.

Key Terms
وَكِيل (wakīl): Agent: a person authorized to act on behalf of another in a legal contract.
طَرَفَي النِّكَاحِ (ṭarafay al-nikāḥ): Both sides of the marriage contract, i.e., the groom and the bride (or their guardians).
حُقُوق (ḥuqūq): Rights and obligations that arise from a contract; in sale these include payment and delivery, which attach to the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because in sale, rights and obligations attach to the agent, creating a contradiction if the same person represents both sides; in marriage, rights do not attach to the agent.
C) Because marriage requires witnesses while sale does not.
D) Because the agent must be a male in sale but not in marriage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, one person can validly act as agent for both parties in marriage because rights do not attach to the agent, unlike in sale where rights attach and make dual agency impossible.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner
6 min
Dual Agency in Marriage vs. Sale
States principle and explains the difference through rights allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi distinction between agency in marriage (nikah) and agency in sale (bayʿ) based on the allocation of rights and apply it to the 'zawwijini' scenario.

Core Idea
In nikah, the wakil is a mere messenger; in bayʿ, the wakil bears rights, preventing dual agency.

Imagine a friend asks you to represent both sides in a sale – it would be impossible because you'd owe payment and delivery to yourself. But in marriage, the same arrangement is perfectly valid. Why the difference? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning from al-Hidayah, revealing how the nature of rights in each contract determines the permissibility of dual agency. By the end, you'll not only know the rule but also the underlying logic that makes the distinction legally sound.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Identify the core ruling: a single person can act as agent (wakil) for both the man and the woman in a marriage contract. Contrast this with sale, where one person cannot represent both buyer and seller.
The Arabic phrase 'khilāf al-bayʿ' signals that sale is the exceptional case.
2
Identify the illa (effective cause)
According to the Hanafi school, the difference stems from how rights are allocated. In a sale, rights like receiving payment and delivering the object revert to the wakil, making him personally liable. In marriage, the wakil is merely a safīr (messenger) – rights such as mahr are due between the spouses, not the wakil.
This illa is extracted by tahrīr al-manāṭ: observing that the wakil in sale is a party to rights while in nikah he is not.
3
Analyse the qiyas
The ruling for marriage is derived through qiyas with sale as the aṣl. The illa (rights revert to wakil) is jaliyyah (clear). Since the illa is absent in nikah, the ruling is different: dual agency is valid.
The qiyas panel in this lesson visualises this reasoning.
4
Apply to the 'zawwijini' scenario
A man says to a woman 'Zawwijini' (marry me). She then marries herself to him. The man’s statement appoints her as his wakil while she remains the principal on her side. This is valid because it is dual agency in nikah, not sale.
The author of al-Hidayah uses this case to illustrate the practical effect of the rule.
5
Contrast with sale
If the same scenario occurred in a sale – e.g., 'Sell my car to yourself' – the contract would be invalid because the wakil would be both claimant and defendant (muṭālib wa muṭālab).
This contrast reinforces the distinction based on rights allocation.

Book of Nikah. (1) Indeed, one person undertakes both sides of marriage, contrary to sale. Translation: A single person can be the representative on both sides of marriage, unlike sale. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that one person can be an agent (wakil) for both parties in marriage, i.e., for both the man and the woman, unlike sale where one person cannot be an agent for both parties, i.e., the seller and the buyer, because in sale all rights revert to the agent, e.g., handing over the sold item and receiving payment, etc., become the agent's responsibility. Now if one person becomes agent for both the seller and the buyer, then a single person would become both the demander and the demanded, and a single person being both demander and demanded is invalid, so agency from both sides in sale is also invalid. In other words, an agent in sale is both the one who makes someone owner and the one who takes ownership. But in marriage it is not like this, because in marriage rights do not revert to the agent, e.g., paying the mahr etc. is not the agent's responsibility; rather, the woman will demand the mahr from her husband herself. Therefore, in marriage if one person becomes agent from both sides, the demander and demanded being one person does not necessarily occur, so agency from both sides in marriage is valid. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, intends to convey that if a person says to a woman: 'Zawwijini' (marry me), and the woman then marries herself to him, the marriage is contracted because in this case the man appointed the woman as his agent for marriage, i.e., the man by saying 'Zawwijini' effectively told her: 'You, as principal on your side and as my agent, contract my marriage with yourself.' So this is agency from both sides in marriage, and agency from both sides in marriage is valid, therefore the marriage is contracted. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, volume 2, page 325, Rahmaniyya edition. And the difference between marriage and sale is that rights in sale pertain to the agent, and the agent in marriage is not (incomplete).
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the book of Nikah in al-Hidayah. It establishes the principle that a single person can act as agent (wakil) for both parties in a marriage contract, unlike in a sale contract. The key distinction is based on how rights are allocated. In sale, all rights (like receiving payment and delivering goods) revert to the agent, so if one person acts for both seller and buyer, that person would be both demanding and being demanded, which is impossible. In marriage, rights such as mahr are not the agent's responsibility; they are between the spouses directly. Therefore, an agent in marriage is merely a messenger (safir) or intermediary, not a party to the rights. Hence, it is valid for one person to represent both the man and the woman. The commentator applies this to the case where a man tells a woman 'Zawwijini' (marry me) and she marries herself to him: since she acts as both principal on her side and agent on his side, the marriage is valid. The Arabic text at the end from al-Hidayah explicitly states the difference: rights in sale pertain to the agent, while in marriage they do not.

Key Terms
وَكِيل (wakīl): An agent who acts on behalf of another in a contract; in nikah, the wakil is merely a messenger (safir) without personal rights or liabilities.
مُطَالِب وَمُطَالَب (muṭālib wa muṭālab): Lit. demander and demanded; refers to the impossibility of one person being both the party who claims and the party against whom the claim is made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a single agent can represent both parties in marriage because the agent bears no personal rights, unlike in sale.

Sababiyyah: The Path of Metaphor in Hanafi Nikah
Defines sababiyyah as a basis for metaphorical usage and applies to tamlik in nikah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle of sababiyyah, apply it to explain why tamlik is a valid expression for marriage in the Hanafi school, and identify the Shafi'i counter-position.

Core Idea
A cause can be used metaphorically for its effect; thus tamlik, a cause of conjugal rights, validly contracts marriage.

In this passage, the author of al-Hidayah establishes a key linguistic principle: causality (sababiyyah) is a valid basis for metaphorical usage. This principle is then employed to justify the Hanafi position that words like tamlik (transfer of ownership) and hibah (gift) can effect a valid marriage contract, even though they literally denote property transactions. Understanding this principle is essential for appreciating how the Hanafi school expands the scope of valid contract expressions.

Guided Path
1
State the principle of sababiyyah
Identify the rule: using a cause (sabab) to refer to its effect (musabbab) is a valid type of metaphor (majaz). This is the core linguistic tool.
The text explicitly says: 'Indeed, causality is the path of metaphor.'
2
Trace the causal chain from tamlik to milk al-mut'ah
In the context of a slave-woman, tamlik (transfer of ownership of her person, milk al-raqabah) causes the acquirer to also obtain milk al-mut'ah (right to sexual enjoyment). So tamlik is a cause for milk al-mut'ah.
Milk al-mut'ah is normally achieved through nikah. The connection makes tamlik a cause for the effect of marriage rights.
3
Apply sababiyyah to the marriage contract
Since tamlik is a cause for milk al-mut'ah, and a cause can be used metaphorically to mean its effect, the word tamlik can be used metaphorically to mean the marriage contract itself.
Thus, saying 'I tamlik you' (I transfer ownership) can be understood as 'I marry you'.
4
Recognize the Hanafi position and the cross-madhhab disagreement
The Hanafi school holds that marriage is valid with tamlik or hibah. The Shafi'i school disagrees, requiring explicit words like nikah or tazwij.
The disagreement stems from different acceptance of metaphorical extension via sababiyyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۲) إِنَّ السَّبَبِيَّةَ طَرِيقُ الْمَجَاز .
ترجمہ: سبیت مجاز کا راستہ ہے۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ سبب بول کر مسبب مراد لینا از قبیل مجاز ہے اس لئے کہ سبب ومسبب کے درمیان مناسبت ہوتی ہے، بایں طور کہ مسبب اپنے وجود میں سبب کا محتاج ہوتا ہے، لہذ اسبب بول کر مسبب مراد لینا درست ہے۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو بتلا رہے ہیں کہ لفظ تملیک اور ہبہ کے الفاظ کے ساتھ احناف کے نزدیک نکاح منعقد ہو جاتا ہے، اس لئے کہ تملیک ملک رقبہ کے واسطے سے ملک متعہ کا سبب ہے، مثلاً اگر کوئی شخص باندی کے ملک رقبہ کا مالک بن گیا تو اس کے ضمن میں ملک متعہ یعنی بضعہ کا مالک بھی بن جائے گا۔ ملک متعہ حقیقتا تو نکاح سے حاصل ہوتا ہے، مگر باندی میں اس کا حصول تملیک سے ہو رہا ہے، تو گویا تملیک حصولِ ملک متعہ کا سبب ٹھہری اور بطریق مجاز اس سے وہ چیز حاصل ہو گئی ( متعہ ) جو نکاح سے اصلاً ثابت اور حاصل ہوتی ہے، لہذا جب تملیک حصولِ ملک متعہ کا سبب ٹھہری اور سبیت مجاز کا علاقہ ہے تو تملیک وغیرہ نکاح اور تزویج کے مجازی معنی ہوں گے ، جس طرح حقیقی معنی ادا کرنے والے الفاظ یعنی نکاح اور تزویج سے نکاح منعقد ہو جاتا ہے، اسی طرح نکاح کے مجازی معنی ادا کرنے والے الفاظ مثلاً تملیک اور ہبہ وغیرہ سے بھی نکاح منعقد ہو جائے گا۔
= كذلك لأنه لا يطالب بتسليم المهر ولا غيره.
البناية: كتاب النكاح، ج ۵ ص ۷، ط: دار الكتب العلمية أَوَّلُهَا أَنَّ الْحُقُوق فِي الْبَيْعِ تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْوَكِيلِ فَلَوْ تَوَلَّى الْوَاحِدُ طَرَفَى الْبَيْعِ أَفْضَى إِلَى السَّنَافِي، وَلَا كَذَلِكَ النِّكَاحُ، فَإِنَّ وَكِيلَ الزَّوْجِ لَا يُطَالِبُ بِتَسْلِيمِ الْمَهْرِ، وَلَا غَيْرِهِ مِنْ حُقُوقِهِ لِأَنَّهُ سَفِيرٌ
فِيهِ، وَكَذَا وَكِيلُ الزَّوْجَةِ لَا يُطَالِبُ بِتَسْلِيمِهَا بَلْ هُوَ سَفِيرٌ وَمُعَبِّرٌ عَنْهَا كَذَا فِي الْغَايَةِ. حاشية الشلبي على تبيين الحقائق كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۹۶، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية الهداية: كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۳۲۵، ط: رحمانیه
التمليك سبب لملك المتعة وهو الثابت أى ملك الرقبة وهو الثابت (بالنكاح)
(2) Indeed, causality (sababiyyah) is the path of metaphor. Translation: Causality is the path of metaphor. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that using a cause to intend the effect is a type of metaphor, because there is a connection between cause and effect, in that the effect is dependent on the cause for its existence. Therefore, it is valid to use the cause to mean the effect. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, intends to convey that with the words tamlik (transfer of ownership) and hibah (gift), according to the Hanafis, marriage is contracted, because tamlik is a cause for milk al-mut'ah (right to sexual enjoyment) via milk al-raqabah (ownership of the slave-woman). For example, if a person becomes the owner of a slave-woman's milk al-raqabah (the right of ownership over her person), then incidentally he also becomes the owner of milk al-mut'ah, i.e., her sexual right. Milk al-mut'ah is originally obtained through marriage, but in the case of a slave-woman, its acquisition occurs through tamlik. So tamlik became the cause for acquiring milk al-mut'ah, and by way of metaphor, that thing (milk al-mut'ah) which is originally established and obtained through marriage is obtained from it. Therefore, when tamlik is a cause for acquiring milk al-mut'ah, and causality is the domain of metaphor, then tamlik etc. are metaphorical meanings of nikah and tazwij. Just as marriage is contracted with words that convey the literal meaning, i.e., nikah and tazwij, similarly, marriage will be contracted with words that convey the metaphorical meaning of nikah, such as tamlik and hibah. = Likewise, because he is not required to deliver the mahr or anything else. Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 5, p. 7, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah: 'The first of them is that rights in sale pertain to the agent, so if one person undertakes both sides of sale, it leads to contradiction, but not so in marriage. For the husband's agent is not required to deliver mahr or any other rights, because he is a messenger in it. Likewise, the wife's agent is not required to deliver her, but rather he is a messenger and spokesperson for her as stated in al-Ghayah.' Hashiyat al-Shulbi on Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 96, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 325, Rahmaniyya. Tamlik is a cause for milk al-mut'ah, and it (milk al-mut'ah) is established, meaning milk al-raqabah, and it is established (by nikah).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of sababiyyah (causality) as a basis for metaphorical usage in legal language. It states that using a cause (sabab) to refer to its effect (musabbab) is a form of metaphor (majaz). The commentator applies this to the issue of marriage: The Hanafi school holds that words like tamlik (transfer of ownership) and hibah (gift) can effect a marriage contract, even though they literally denote transfer of property. The reasoning is that tamlik of a slave-woman's milk al-raqabah (ownership of her person) is a cause for acquiring milk al-mut'ah (right to sexual enjoyment), which is normally achieved through nikah. Thus, the word tamlik is used metaphorically to mean nikah. The text supports this by quoting from al-Binayah and al-Hidayah, providing additional evidence from the discussion on agency in marriage versus sale. The phrase 'al-tamlik sabab li milk al-mut'ah' is specifically highlighted as a basis for this metaphorical usage.

Key Terms
سَبَبِيَّة (sababiyyah): The relationship of causality; using a cause to refer to its effect is a valid type of metaphor (majaz).
مِلْك الْمُتْعَة (milk al-mut'ah): The right to sexual enjoyment; in marriage, it is the primary object of the contract. In the case of a slave-woman, it is acquired through ownership of her person (milk al-raqabah).
تَمْلِيك (tamlik): Literally 'transfer of ownership'; used as a metaphorical expression for marriage in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi school justify using the word 'tamlik' to effect marriage?
A) By analogy with sale
B) Through the principle of sababiyyah, where a cause can metaphorically refer to its effect
C) By direct Quranic command
D) Only in cases of necessity

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the principle of sababiyyah to validate marriage contracts expressed through cause-words like tamlik and hibah.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Whoever Has Guardianship Has Qualification for Testimony: The Case of the Fasiq Witness
Establishing that a fasiq may serve as a witness because he has guardianship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a fasiq retains guardianship and is qualified to be a witness in marriage, and distinguish this from the Shafi'i requirement of 'adalah.

Core Idea
Fisq does not strip guardianship, so it does not disqualify a person from being a marriage witness in the Hanafi school.

Marriage requires witnesses, but what if those witnesses are sinners? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools reach opposite conclusions. At the heart of the dispute is a powerful legal maxim: whoever is capable of guardianship is also capable of testimony. Let's explore how this maxim works and why the Hanafi school holds that a fasiq can still witness a marriage.

Guided Path
1
State the competing positions
Identify the Hanafi position (marriage valid with fasiq witnesses) and the Shafi'i position (invalid unless witnesses are 'udul).
The Hanafi position relies on the hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' without additional conditions.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the two pillars of the Hanafi proof: (1) the absolute hadith, (2) the qawida that guardianship implies testimony. Understand why fisq does not negate guardianship.
Guardianship over oneself is retained despite sin; hence testimony, which is a lesser capacity, is also retained.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school analogizes marriage witnesses to court witnesses, where 'adalah is required. They argue that the same moral reliability is needed.
This qiyas is rejected by Hanafis because marriage is a private contract, not a judicial proceeding.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that fisq does not remove a person's ability to exercise guardianship, so the analogy fails. The hadith is absolute and does not require 'adalah.
The qawida 'whoever is of the people of guardianship is of the people of testimony' is considered stronger than qiyas.
5
Identify the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In the Hanafi school, a marriage with two fasiq witnesses is valid; in the Shafi'i school, it is invalid unless witnesses repent and become 'udul.
This affects the legal status of many marriages in communities where upright witnesses may be hard to find.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَنْ كَانَ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْوِلَايَةِ يَكُونُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الشَّهَادَةِ. ترجمہ: جو شخص اہل ولایت میں سے ہو تو وہ اہلِ شہادت میں سے بھی ہو گا۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جوشخص ولایت کا اہل ہو یعنی جس کو اپنی ذات پر ولایت حاصل ہو تو اہل شہادت بھی ہو گا یعنی گواہ بننے کا اہل بھی ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ احناف کے نزدیک دو فاسق آدمیوں کی موجودگی میں نکاح منعقد ہو جائے گا، اس لئے کہ فاسق کا فسق و فجور اس کی ذات تک محدود ہے، دیگر لوگ اس سے مستثنی ہیں ، یہی وجہ ہے کہ شریعت نے اسے فسق کے باجود اہلِ ولایت میں سے مانا ہے یعنی وہ خود اپنا نکاح کر سکتا ہے اور اپنے غلام ، باندی وغیرہ کا بھی نکاح کر سکتا ہے، تو جس طرح فاسق کے لئے ولایت ثابت ہے، اسی طرح شہادت بھی ثابت ہوگی ، اور اہلِ ولایت میں سے ہونے کے ساتھ ساتھ وہ اہلِ شہادت میں سے بھی ہوگا، جب شریعت نے فاسق کو اپنی ذات پر ولایت سے محروم نہیں کیا تو وہ غیر پر بھی ولایت سے محروم نہیں ہوگا، اس لئے کہ وہ غیر بھی مسلمان ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ہم جنس ہے، تو جب انسان اپنے اوپر ولایت کا مالک ہے تو اپنے ہم جنس پر بھی ولایت کا مالک ہوگا۔ = فكان بينهما اتصال باعتبار السبب والسببية طريق المجاز وإن لم يكن اتصالا من جهة المعنى. فصحت الاستعارة فيكون من باب إطلاق السبب على المسبب. البناية : كتاب النكاح، ج ۵ ص ا ا ، ط: دار الكتب العلمية (قَوْلُهُ: وَالسَّبَيَّةُ) فَإِذَا ثَبَتَ أَنَّ تِلْكَ الْأَلْفَاظَ سَبَبٌ لِمِلْكِ الْمُتْعَةِ عِنْدَ عَدَمِ الْمَانِعِ الشَّرْعِيِّ فَإِطْلَاقُ السَّبَبِ، وَإِرَادَةُ الْمُسَبِّبِ مِنْ أَقْوَى طُرُقِ الْمَجَازِ. حاشية الشلبي على تبيين الحقائق كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۹۷، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية الهداية: كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۳۲۶، ط: رحمانیه مسألة: ينعقد النكاح بحضور الشهود وإن كانوا غير عدول عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله وعند الشافعي لا ينعقد بحضوة فاسقين. حجة أبى حنيفة رحمه الله : إطلاق قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: لا نكاح إلا بشهود "من غير =
Whoever is qualified for guardianship is also qualified for testimony. Translation: Anyone who is of the people of guardianship will also be of the people of testimony. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that a person who qualifies for guardianship, i.e., who has guardianship over himself, will also be qualified for testimony, i.e., he will be eligible to be a witness. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is establishing that according to the Hanafis, marriage is concluded in the presence of two sinful men, because the sinfulness of a fasiq is limited to his own person; others are excluded from it. This is why the Law considers him, despite his sin, to be among the people of guardianship—i.e., he can conduct his own marriage and also the marriage of his slave, slave-girl, etc. Thus, just as guardianship is established for the fasiq, so too is testimony established, and along with being of the people of guardianship, he will also be of the people of testimony. Since the Law has not deprived the fasiq of guardianship over his own person, he will not be deprived of guardianship over others, because that other person, due to being a Muslim, is of his same kind. So when a person possesses guardianship over himself, he will also possess guardianship over his own kind. = So there is connection between them by virtue of the cause, and causality is the path of metaphor, even if there is no connection in meaning. Thus the metaphorical usage is correct, and it falls under the category of using the cause for the effect. Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 5, p. 11, Dar al-Kutub al-'Ilmiyyah (His saying: 'and the causality') So when it is established that those words are a cause for ownership of usufruct in the absence of a legal prevention, then using the cause and intending the effect is among the strongest paths of metaphor. Hashiyat al-Shilbi 'ala Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 97, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah. Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 326, Rahmaniyyah. Issue: Marriage is concluded in the presence of witnesses even if they are not upright ('udul) according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). According to al-Shafi'i, it is not concluded in the presence of two sinners. The proof of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) is the general application of his statement, peace be upon him: 'No marriage except with witnesses' without...
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents another qawida: 'مَنْ كَانَ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْوِلَايَةِ يَكُونُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الشَّهَادَةِ' (whoever is capable of guardianship is also capable of testimony). The author uses this principle to support the Hanafi position that a marriage contract is valid even if the two witnesses are fasiq (sinful). The reasoning is that a fasiq retains guardianship (wilayah) over himself and his dependents, so his deficiency (fisq) does not negate his capacity to act as a witness. The Shafi'i school requires witnesses to be 'udul (upright). Imam Abu Hanifa's proof is the hadith 'La nikaha illa bi shuhud' (No marriage except with witnesses), which does not specify the condition of 'adalah (uprightness). The passage also includes a reminder of the earlier qawida about causation and metaphor, but the main focus is on the fasiq witness issue. The author stresses that sinfulness does not strip a person of all legal capacity; he can still perform his own marriage and that of his slaves, so he can witness.

Key Terms
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or legal authority over oneself or others, e.g., to contract marriage.
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony given in court or in marriage; qualification to be a witness.
فاسق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; legally deficient but not stripped of all capacity.
عدول ('udul): Upright, morally reliable witnesses (plural of 'adil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a marriage valid if witnessed by two fasiq (sinful) men?
A) Because the fasiq are not required to be Muslim.
B) Because the fasiq have guardianship over themselves, and the hadith only requires witnesses, not necessarily upright ones.
C) Because the Shafi'i school agrees.
D) Because the Quran explicitly permits it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a fasiq retains guardianship and can be a marriage witness; the hadith does not require uprightness, so the marriage is valid.

Nikah — Marriage

Wilayah Implies Shahadah: Validity of Fasiq Witnesses in Nikah
Principle linking wilayah to shahadah, applied to fasiq marriage witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage witnessed by fasiq persons and explain the principle that wilayah implies shahadah.

Core Idea
If a person has legal capacity over himself, he is fit to witness a marriage — even if sinful.

In this lesson, we examine a core Hanafi principle that links guardianship (wilayah) to witnessing (shahadah). The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to justify why marriage is valid even when witnesses are sinful (fasiq). This distinguishes the Hanafi position from the Shafi’i requirement of ‘adalah (uprightness). Understanding this principle reveals how the Hanafi school prioritises broad legal capacity (ahliyyah) over moral status in certain contractual contexts.

Guided Path
1
State the principle
Identify the maxim: 'Whoever is among the people of wilayah is among the people of shahadah.' Understand that wilayah includes capacity over oneself and over others.
The principle is axiomatic in Hanafi nikah law.
2
Apply the principle to fasiq witnesses
Explain that a fasiq retains wilayah over himself (e.g., he can contract his own marriage), so he is qualified for shahadah. Therefore, his testimony in a marriage contract is acceptable.
The fasiq's sinfulness does not strip him of basic legal capacity.
3
Locate the proof
The primary proof is the hadith: 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' (No marriage except with witnesses). The hadith does not qualify 'witnesses' with 'adalah, so the default is that any Muslim with basic capacity suffices.
This is a general command ('amm); adding the condition of 'adalah would be takhsis without evidence.
4
Contrast with the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i requires witnesses to be 'adil (upright). He interprets the hadith as implicitly requiring 'adalah, possibly by analogy to court testimony.
The Shafi'i ruling makes marriage with fasiq witnesses invalid.
5
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi school validates the marriage; Shafi'i school invalidates it, requiring a new marriage contract with upright witnesses.
This difference affects the validity of existing marriages and the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: جو شخص اہل ولایت میں سے ہو تو وہ اہلِ شہادت میں سے بھی ہو گا۔
تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جوشخص ولایت کا اہل ہو یعنی جس کو اپنی ذات پر ولایت حاصل ہو تو اہل شہادت بھی ہو گا یعنی گواہ بننے کا اہل بھی ہوگا۔
صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ احناف کے نزدیک دو فاسق آدمیوں کی موجودگی میں نکاح منعقد ہو جائے گا، اس لئے کہ فاسق کا فسق و فجور اس کی ذات تک محدود ہے، دیگر لوگ اس سے مستثنی ہیں ، یہی وجہ ہے کہ شریعت نے اسے فسق کے باجود اہلِ ولایت میں سے مانا ہے یعنی وہ خود اپنا نکاح کر سکتا ہے اور اپنے غلام ، باندی وغیرہ کا بھی نکاح کر سکتا ہے، تو جس طرح فاسق کے لئے ولایت ثابت ہے، اسی طرح شہادت بھی ثابت ہوگی ، اور اہلِ ولایت میں سے ہونے کے ساتھ ساتھ وہ اہلِ شہادت میں سے بھی ہوگا، جب شریعت نے فاسق کو اپنی ذات پر ولایت سے محروم نہیں کیا تو وہ غیر پر بھی ولایت سے محروم نہیں ہوگا، اس لئے کہ وہ غیر بھی مسلمان ہونے کی وجہ سے اس کا ہم جنس ہے، تو جب انسان اپنے اوپر ولایت کا مالک ہے تو اپنے ہم جنس پر بھی ولایت کا مالک ہوگا۔
= فكان بينهما اتصال باعتبار السبب والسببية طريق المجاز وإن لم يكن اتصالا من جهة المعنى. فصحت الاستعارة فيكون من باب إطلاق السبب على المسبب.
البناية : كتاب النكاح، ج ۵ ص ا ا ، ط: دار الكتب العلمية (قَوْلُهُ: وَالسَّبَيَّةُ) فَإِذَا ثَبَتَ أَنَّ تِلْكَ الْأَلْفَاظَ سَبَبٌ لِمِلْكِ الْمُتْعَةِ عِنْدَ عَدَمِ الْمَانِعِ الشَّرْعِيِّ فَإِطْلَاقُ السَّبَبِ، وَإِرَادَةُ الْمُسَبِّبِ مِنْ أَقْوَى طُرُقِ الْمَجَازِ.
حاشية الشلبي على تبيين الحقائق كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۹۷، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية الهداية: كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۳۲۶، ط: رحمانیه

وعند الشافعي لا ينعقد بحضرة فاسقين.

(3) Whoever is among the people of wilayah (guardianship) is among the people of shahadah (witnessing).
Translation: The person who is qualified for wilayah, i.e., who has guardianship over his own self, will also be qualified for shahadah, i.e., fit to be a witness.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that whoever is qualified for wilayah, i.e., has guardianship over his own self, will also be qualified for shahadah, i.e., fit to be a witness.
The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that according to the Hanafis, marriage is contracted in the presence of two fasiq (sinful) men, because the sinfulness of a fasiq is limited to his own person; other people are exempt from it. This is why the Shari'ah considers him, despite his sinfulness, as being among the people of wilayah, meaning he can perform his own marriage and can also perform the marriage of his slave, slave-girl, etc. Thus, just as wilayah is established for the fasiq, similarly shahadah is also established, and along with being among the people of wilayah, he is also among the people of shahadah. When the Shari'ah has not deprived the fasiq of wilayah over his own self, then he will not be deprived of wilayah over others, because the other person, by virtue of being Muslim, is of his same kind. So when a person has ownership of wilayah over himself, he will also have ownership of wilayah over his same kind.
= For between them there is connection in terms of cause and causality, the way of metaphor, even if there is no connection in terms of meaning. So the metaphor is valid, and it is from the chapter of applying the cause to the effect.
Al-Binayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 5, p. [؟], Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah (His statement: 'and the captive woman') So when it is established that those words are a cause for ownership of enjoyment in the absence of a legal impediment, then applying the cause and intending the effect is among the strongest ways of metaphor.
Hashiyat al-Shalabi 'ala Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 97, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah.
Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 326, al-Rahmaniyyah.
Issue: Marriage is contracted with the presence of witnesses even if they are not upright ('udul) according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him). According to al-Shafi'i, it is not contracted with the presence of two fasiqs.
The proof of Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): The general meaning of his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no marriage except with witnesses' from without [؟] =
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi legal principle and applies it to the validity of marriage witnesses. The principle 'Whoever is among the people of wilayah is among the people of shahadah' means that a person's capacity for guardianship over himself (like performing his own marriage) establishes his capacity for witnessing. The commentator explains that the author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to prove that two fasiq witnesses suffice for a valid marriage in the Hanafi school. The reasoning is that a fasiq's sinfulness does not remove his basic legal capacity (ahliyyah) for wilayah over himself; since he can marry himself and manage his own affairs, he remains qualified for wilayah over others of the same kind (i.e., Muslims). Therefore, his testimony is accepted, as shahadah follows from wilayah. The passage then cites references from al-Binayah and Hashiyah, and presents a mas'ala (ruling) contrasting Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i: Abu Hanifa holds marriage valid with non-‘adil witnesses, while al-Shafi'i requires ‘adil witnesses. The proof is the general hadith 'لا نكاح إلا بشهود' (There is no marriage except with witnesses), which does not specify ‘adalah. The incomplete citation likely continues with a qualification like 'without public announcement' but the meaning is clear: the command is general, and any specification beyond what the text provides is a restriction (takhsis) without evidence, which the Hanafis reject.

Key Terms
ولایت (wilayah): Legal guardianship or authority; here specifically the capacity to act on one’s own behalf or on behalf of others in marriage contracting.
شہادت (shahadah): The act of testifying or giving legal testimony; being a witness in a court or contract, especially marriage.
فاسق (fasiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, but is still a Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the ruling on a marriage contract witnessed by two fasiq (sinful) men?
A) The marriage is invalid because the witnesses are not upright.
B) The marriage is valid because the witnesses are Muslims and have basic legal capacity.
C) The marriage is suspended until the witnesses repent.
D) The marriage requires additional witnesses to compensate.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates marriage with fasiq witnesses based on the principle that wilayah implies shahadah, and the general hadith does not require 'adalah.

The Principle of Greater Includes Lesser: Fasiq Witness in Marriage
Presents a principle of logical priority applied to fasiq's witness capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that capacity for a higher function implies capacity for a lower one, and apply it to argue that a fasiq is a valid witness in marriage.

Core Idea
If a fasiq can appoint a qadi, he can be a witness – the greater includes the lesser.

This passage from al-Hidayah introduces a key Hanafi principle: whoever is fit to appoint a qadi (a higher task) is fit to be a qadi or witness (lower tasks). The author uses this principle to address a disputed point: whether a fasiq (sinful) person can be a valid witness in a marriage contract. Let's unpack the logic and see how it flows from the concept of wilayah (authority) and the shared characteristic of tanfidh al-qawl (enforcing one's statement over others).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
The principle states: 'Whoever is fit to appoint a qadi (muqallid) is fit to be a qadi and also a witness.' Appointing a qadi is considered a higher function than being a qadi or a witness. The idea is that capacity for the higher necessarily includes capacity for the lower.
This is a logical a fortiori (awlawi) argument.
2
Recognize the Application to Fasiq
Apply the principle to a fasiq ruler. If a fasiq has the authority to appoint a qadi (as a ruler or sultan), then by the principle he is fit to be a qadi and a witness. The author stresses that many historical rulers had fisq, yet they exercised the power to appoint judges.
The fasiq's authority (wilayah) is the key – fisq does not negate it.
3
Connect via Common Element (Tanfidh al-Qawl)
The principle works because both qada' (judgeship) and shahadah (witnessing) share the characteristic of 'tanfidh al-qawl `ala al-ghayr' – enforcing one's statement over others. Appointing a qadi also involves this element. Thus, capacity for the greater (appointing) proves capacity for the lesser (being a witness).
This common element is a Hanafi conceptual tool to link the roles.

(2) Indeed, whoever is fit to appoint a qadi (muqallid) is fit to be a qadi and also a witness.
Translation: A person who has the capacity to appoint a qadi also has the capacity to be a qadi and a witness.
Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that a person who has the capacity to appoint another as qadi also has the capacity to be a qadi and a witness himself, because appointing a qadi is a higher matter than being a qadi. Thus, if he has capacity for the higher, he most certainly has capacity for the lower. The author of al-Hidayah mentions this principle to inform that in marriage contracts, according to the Hanafis, a fasiq person can be a witness, because if a fasiq is a ruler, he has the capacity to appoint another as qadi, and whoever has the capacity to appoint another as qadi can himself be a qadi, and if he can be a qadi, he can also be a witness, because shahadah and qada' belong to the same category – both involve enforcing a statement over another (tanfidh al-qawl ‘ala al-ghayr).
= Limitation: and because the fasiq is from the people of wilayah, he is from the people of shahadah; and because he is fit to be an imam and a sultan, so he is fit to be a qadi and a witness as a matter of priority.
Al-Ghurra al-Munifa: Kitab al-Nikah, p. 133, Mu'assasat al-Kutub al-Thaqafiyyah.
And our proof is that he is from the people of wilayah, so he is from the people of shahadah; this is because when wilayah is not prohibited for him concerning himself, it is not prohibited concerning others, because they are of his kind; and because he is from the people of the supreme imamate, and from the necessity of being qualified for it is that he is qualified for qada', and from that follows that he is qualified for shahadah.
Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 99, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah.
Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2, p. 326, al-Rahmaniyyah.

Commentary
This passage introduces another principle from al-Hidayah: the capacity to appoint a qadi implies the capacity to be a qadi and a witness. The logic is that appointing a qadi (a higher function) requires more qualification than being a qadi or a witness; therefore, if someone is qualified to appoint, he is certainly qualified for the lesser roles. The author applies this to the case of a fasiq witness in marriage: if a fasiq ruler can appoint judges, he himself can be a judge and a witness, because qada' and shahadah both involve influencing others (tanfidh al-qawl). The Arabic sources (al-Ghurra al-Munifa, Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, al-Hidayah) reinforce that the fasiq is from ahl al-wilayah and capable of imamate, so by priority he is fit for qada' and shahadah. The passage ends with a discussion on the grave corruption that would result if leadership were denied to anyone with fisq, implying that many historical leaders were not sinless. The incomplete text likely continues to argue that since the fasiq is still a Muslim, and Islamic rule would collapse if fisq disqualified rulers, the witness’s sin is irrelevant.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): One who appoints a qadi (a ruler or leader who delegates judicial authority).
قضاء (qada'): Judgeship; the function of a qadi in adjudicating disputes and enforcing rulings.
تنفيذ القول على الغير (tanfidh al-qawl `ala al-ghayr): The enforcement of one's statement upon another; the common element between being a judge and a witness.

Key Takeaway
Capacity for a higher function (appointing a qadi) necessarily includes capacity for a lower function (being a witness), justifying the acceptance of a fasiq witness in marriage.

The Fasiq as Witness in Marriage: The Wilayah Principle
Hanafi ruling with proof: marriage witnesses and wilaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits a fasiq to serve as a witness in marriage, using the qawida that appointee qualification implies personal qualification.

Core Idea
A fasiq can witness marriage because he has wilayah – the capacity to appoint a judge.

This lesson introduces a core Hanafi legal principle: whoever is qualified to appoint a judge is also qualified to be a judge and a witness. The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to justify the permissibility of a fasiq (sinner) as a witness in marriage – a clear departure from the Shafi'i requirement of uprightness. Understanding this principle is key to grasping how Hanafi jurisprudence links authority and witnessing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida
The principle: 'Whoever is qualified to appoint a judge is qualified to be a judge and witness.' Recognise this as a fiqh maxim (qā'ida fiqhiyya).
2
Understand the a fortiori reasoning
Appointing a judge is superior to being a judge or witness. If one is capable of the superior act, by primary inference (awlā) one is capable of the inferior.
3
Apply to the fasiq
A fasiq (e.g., a ruler) has wilāyah (authority). He can appoint a judge, therefore he can be a judge and a witness.
4
Note the practical ruling
In the Hanafi school, a marriage contracted with fasiq witnesses is valid (sahih). The witnesses do not need to be 'adl (upright).
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school requires witnesses to be 'adl. The Hanafi view is based on a broader definition of ahl al-shahādah through wilāyah.

Source Text

الغرة المنيفة : كتاب النكاح، ص ۱۳۳ ، ط: مؤسسة الكتب الثقافية
وَلَنَا أَنَّهُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْوِلَايَةِ فَيَكُونُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الشَّهَادَةِ وَهَذَا لِأَنَّهُ لَمَّا لَمْ تَحْرُمُ الْوِلَايَةُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ لَا تَحْرُمُ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ، لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ جِنْسِهِ وَلَأَنَّهُ مِنْ أَهْلِ الْإِمَامَةِ الْكُبْرَى وَمِنْ ضَرُورَةِ كَوْنِهِ أَهْلَا لَهَا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَهْلًا لِلْقَضَاءِ وَيَلْزَمُ مِنْهُ أَنْ يَكُونَ أَهْلَا لِلشَّهَادَةِ.
تبيين الحقائق: كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۹۹ ، ط: المطبعة الكبرى الأميرية
الهداية كتاب النكاح، ج ۲ ص ۳۲۶، ط: رحمانیه
(ولأنه أى ولأن الفاسق وهو الشاهد صلح مقلدا بكسر اللام، أى سلطانا وخليفة، فإن جمهور الأئمة بعد الخلفاء الأربعة قَلَّمَا يَخُلُو وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمْ عَنْ ، أى المشهود عليه من جنس الفاسق من حيث إن كل واحد منهما مسلم لأنه أى لأن الولاية على تأويل الحكم المذكور ونحوه فالقول بالخروج عن الإمامة بالفسق يفضى إلى فساد عظيم. فإذا كان كذلك
(2) Indeed, whoever is qualified to appoint a judge is qualified to be a judge, and likewise a witness. Translation: A person who has the capability to appoint a judge also has the capability to become a judge himself and to become a witness. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that a person who can appoint another as a judge also has the capability to become a judge himself and a witness, because appointing a judge is superior to being a judge. So if one has the capability for the superior, then by primary inference he has capability for the inferior. The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, informs that in the marriage contract, according to the Hanafis, a sinful person can be a witness, because if a sinful person is a ruler, he has the capability to appoint another as judge, and whoever can appoint another as judge can himself be a judge, and if he can be a judge, then he can also be a witness, because witnessing and judgment are from the same category, since both involve the meaning of 'enforcing one's statement upon others'. = Recording: And because the sinful person is from the people of authority, so he is from the people of witnessing, and because he is qualified as an imam and sultan, so by primary inference he is qualified as a judge and witness. (Al-Ghurrah al-Munifah: Kitab al-Nikah, p. 133, Mu'assasat al-Kutub al-Thaqafiyyah) And we argue that he is from the people of authority, so he is from the people of witnessing. This is because when authority is not prohibited for himself, it is not prohibited for others, because it is of its kind. And because he is from the people of the greater imamate, and from the necessity of his being qualified for it, he is qualified for judgment, and it follows from that that he is qualified for witnessing. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2 p. 99, al-Matba'ah al-Kubra al-Amiriyyah, Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Nikah, vol. 2 p. 326, Rahmaniyyah) (And because i.e. because the sinful person who is the witness is qualified as 'muqallid' with a kasra on the lam, i.e. sultan and caliph, for the majority of imams after the four caliphs rarely is one of them free from [sin], and the one against whom testimony is given is of the same genus as the sinful person in terms of each being Muslim, because i.e. because authority on the interpretation of the mentioned ruling and its like, and the claim of exiting the imamate due to sin leads to great corruption. So if this is the case...
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle (qā‘ida fiqhiyya) from Hanafi jurisprudence: 'Whoever is qualified to appoint a judge is qualified to be a judge and likewise a witness.' The author of Uṣūl al-Hidāyah explains this principle and its application to the issue of witnesses in marriage. The principle relies on an a fortiori (awlā) argument: appointing a judge is a higher responsibility than being a judge or a witness. Therefore, if a person (even a fāsiq) is capable of the higher act (appointing a judge), he is surely capable of the lower acts (being a judge or witness). The passage then establishes the Hanafi ruling that a fāsiq (sinful person) can serve as a witness in a marriage contract. This is because the fāsiq is still a person of wilāyah (authority) in general; he can be a ruler, an imam, or a caliph. If he can be a caliph, then by necessity he can be a judge, and therefore a witness, since judgment (qadā') and witnessing (shahādah) share the same fundamental meaning: enforcing one's statement upon others (tanfīdh al-qawl ‘alā al-ghayr). The author supports this with quotations from Al-Hidāyah, Al-Ghurrah al-Munīfah, and Tabyīn al-Ḥaqā’iq, emphasizing that even sinful rulers are tolerated for the greater good, and excluding them would lead to great corruption. Note: This ruling is specific to marriage; other contracts may have different conditions for witnesses.

Key Terms
مُقَلِدًا (muqallidan): In this context, it means someone who appoints a judge, or someone who is qualified to be a ruler/sultan/caliph who can appoint judges.
فَاسِق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral uprightness (ṣalāḥ).
الْوِلَايَة (al-wilāyah): Authority or guardianship, including the power to appoint judges.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi principle discussed, why is a fāsiq considered qualified to be a witness in marriage?
A) Because a fāsiq is always a just person
B) Because being a fāsiq does not affect one's ability to tell the truth
C) Because a fāsiq has the capacity to appoint a judge, and thus can be a judge and witness by primary inference
D) Because marriage witnesses do not need any qualifications

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a fāsiq as a marriage witness because the capacity for wilāyah implies capacity for testimony through a fortiori reasoning.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
